# 2. Purpose

This artefact preserves full chat material for ESR-0008 as repository-controlled historical evidence under AIEMS.

Preserves the ESR-0008 engineering session review chat material as historical full-chat evidence for ESR-0008 architecture and Engineering Ecosystem Synchronisation context.

The artefact supports Engineering Ecosystem Synchronisation by making full-chat history available from the repository while preserving controlled repository artefacts as the authoritative engineering baseline.

---

# 3. Scope

This artefact records the supplied full chat / engineering session review transcript content for ESR-0008 in Markdown form.

It does not replace the corresponding Engineering Session Report, historical summary record, repository baseline, programme status, controlled registers, standards, architecture artefacts or implementation evidence.

Where this full chat history conflicts with controlled repository artefacts, the controlled repository artefacts take precedence.

---

# 4. Engineering Authority

Repository evidence remains authoritative over historical conversational memory.

This artefact is supporting historic evidence. It may inform WP0 review, continuity checks, behavioural review and engineering context recovery, but it does not approve new implementation scope or modify any accepted baseline.

---

# 5. Source Preservation Notes

The source content was extracted from the supplied `.docx` file and normalized into repository Markdown.

Normalization was limited to Markdown formatting and ASCII-safe text representation. The purpose is preservation and repository review, not reinterpretation.

# 9. Preserved Full Chat Content

Engineering Session ESR-0008 - Please review attach documents - paying close attention to ESR-0007 docs

Engineering Session ESR-0008 is now initialised from my side.

I've reviewed the engineering history available in this conversation and the supplied engineering session material, with particular attention to the evolution through ESR-0007. The most important outcome isn't a single document-it's the direction the project has taken.

My understanding of the current baseline

The project has now moved beyond "designing an AI assistant" into establishing a governed engineering programme with defined roles:

Programme Sponsor (You) - vision, priorities and approvals.

Chief Architect (ChatGPT) - architecture, governance, planning and design authority.

Independent Engineering Auditor (VS Code/Codex) - repository verification, implementation review and engineering challenge.

That separation proved valuable during the earlier repository verification work and should continue.

What I believe ESR-0007 achieved

The engineering programme has largely completed its foundation phase.

The following are effectively established:

AIEMS engineering methodology.

Repository-first engineering.

Independent verification using Codex.

Obsidian as the Engineering Knowledge Workspace (not the source of truth).

Decision Confidence Framework (Idea Hypothesis Verified Approved).

Six enduring engineering principles, including "Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone." and "Verify before deciding. Never confuse assumptions with facts."

Approved project principles fra...

More importantly, the philosophy shifted from creating governance documents to ensuring governance supports product engineering.

I think this is the correct direction.

The most important lesson from ESR-0007

One issue stood out during the previous sessions.

At one point I recommended implementing functionality that had already been implemented in an earlier session because I relied on an incomplete view of the engineering history.

You correctly challenged that recommendation.

That reinforced one of our core principles:

Verify before deciding. Never confuse assumptions with facts.

Approved project principles fra...

Going forward, I intend to continue treating Codex as the implementation auditor and repository truth where appropriate.

What I recommend for ESR-0008

Rather than introducing another governance layer, I recommend ESR-0008 focuses on engineering execution.

My proposed work packages are:

Objective

Engineering Synchronisation (current)

WP1

ESR-0008 closure and handover

One recommendation I'd like to add

Looking back over all the engineering sessions, I think we've reached the point where AIEMS itself should become relatively stable.

From ESR-0008 onwards, I suggest a new operating rule:

No new governance artefact should be created unless it demonstrably improves engineering delivery.

In other words:

Documentation should support development.

Governance should enable development.

Product capability should become the primary measure of progress.

Current Status

AIEMS is established.

The project is ready to continue product engineering, subject to verification of the current repository state.

The repository already contains the implementation work completed during ESR-0007.

Documentation has continued to evolve and now needs synchronising with the implementation before the next feature package.

Correction

During an active Engineering Session, I should have attempted a read-only GitHub repository review automatically before making engineering recommendations.

I should not have waited for you to tell me to connect.

That is now part of the approved Repository-First Engineering Protocol (RFEP) accepted during ESR-0007. It explicitly states:

GitHub is the authoritative engineering evidence source.

Read-only repository inspection is part of normal engineering analysis.

It does not require explicit Programme Sponsor approval each time.

Repository evidence takes precedence over conversational memory.

Project JARIVIS AI - Engineerin...

My understanding going forward

From this point onwards, whenever we are in an active Engineering Session I will operate as follows:

Automatically perform a read-only GitHub repository review whenever it could materially improve the engineering assessment.

Treat the GitHub repository as the primary engineering authority.

Use Obsidian/OSE as engineering context where appropriate.

Produce recommendations based on repository evidence first.

Await your approval only for:

Engineering implementation

Repository modifications

Codex Engineering Execution Packages

Baseline acceptance

Any write operations

This is exactly the operating model approved in ESR-0007.

I also noted another important point

ESR-0007 goes one step further than simply permitting repository access.

It establishes a complete engineering workflow:

Repository Review Engineering Analysis Recommendation Programme Sponsor Approval Engineering Execution Package Codex Implementation Independent GitHub Verification Repository Baseline Acceptance

That workflow is now my default behaviour during Engineering Sessions.

My expectation

I recommend treating the resulting traceability matrix as a controlled engineering artefact in its own right. ESR-0008 - WP1 Commenced

Work Package: Product Vision Recovery & Strategic Alignment

Status: [green] In Progress

Engineering Evidence Hierarchy (v1.0)

For the remainder of Project JARVIS AI, I propose the following evidence hierarchy, which will become my default working model unless superseded by a future AIEMS update:

Priority

Engineering Authority

Purpose

1

GitHub Repository

Authoritative implementation baseline

2

Repository Baselines (RBL)

Approved engineering state

3

Engineering Session Records (ESR)

Engineering decisions and rationale

4

Full Chat Session Archives

Product vision, design discussions, historical recovery

5

Session Summaries

Indexed navigation into historical discussions

6

Current Engineering Session

Active engineering work

7

Conversational Memory

Context only; never authoritative

This formalises the evidence-based approach we've been evolving and ensures recommendations are grounded in verifiable artefacts.

Product Vision Recovery Methodology

I will now analyse the historical material with five specific objectives:

Phase A - Vision Recovery

Recover every significant architectural and product concept discussed throughout the project, regardless of whether it was implemented.

Phase B - Capability Mapping

For each concept, determine whether it is:

Implemented

Partially Implemented

Documented

Planned

Deferred

Missing

Phase C - Architectural Evolution

Track how major ideas evolved across Engineering Sessions, identifying where designs changed, matured, or were superseded.

Phase D - Gap Analysis

Highlight concepts that were approved or discussed but have not yet been represented in the repository.

Phase E - Strategic Recommendations

Recommend the most valuable capabilities to bring forward into the next Engineering Implementation Packages.

Deliverables from WP1

By the conclusion of this work package, I intend to produce a suite of engineering artefacts for review:

Product Vision Traceability Matrix (PVTM) - Mapping historical concepts to implementation status.

Capability Catalogue (CCAT) - Inventory of current and planned JARVIS capabilities.

Engineering Gap Register (EGR) - Missing or partially implemented concepts.

Architectural Roadmap (ARM) - Recommended implementation sequence based on value, dependency, and architectural readiness.

Engineering Implementation Package Recommendation (EIPR) - Prioritised work package(s) for Codex implementation.

Long-Term Benefit

This approach gives us something many software projects lack: complete traceability from the original vision through to the implemented product.

Every significant capability can be traced back to:

the Engineering Session where it was conceived,

the design rationale,

the implementation artefact,

the repository commit,

the verification activity, and

the approved repository baseline.

That level of traceability aligns extremely well with AIEMS and should make future engineering decisions more objective and easier to audit.

Recommendation

As we continue, I recommend creating a controlled AIEMS artefact titled Product Vision Traceability Matrix (PVTM-0001). Rather than being specific to ESR-0008, it would become a living engineering document maintained across future sessions. It would serve as the master index linking vision, architecture, implementation, verification, and repository baselines, and I believe it will become one of the most valuable artefacts in the entire programme.

I will now continue with WP1 using the authorised evidence sources: the GitHub repository as the implementation authority, the ESR records as engineering decision history, and the full chat archives and summaries to recover historical intent.

I would like to extend the idea slightly

While reviewing ESR-0007A, something became apparent.

The project is now generating several different "master indexes":

OSE

Capability Catalogue

Architecture

Repository

Engineering Sessions

Baselines

Vision

These are all related.

Rather than creating isolated indexes, I recommend we build them as a single connected engineering graph.

In other words:

PVTM
 Capability
 Architecture
 Repository
 Sessions
 Decisions
 Verification
 Implementation

That makes OSE much more than a document store-it becomes the project's Engineering Knowledge Graph.

One further recommendation

I would also recommend assigning persistent identifiers to engineering concepts, not just documents.

For example:

Identifier

Meaning

PVTM-0001

Product Vision Traceability Matrix

CAP-0007

Memory Subsystem

CAP-0014

Voice Interface

ADR-0003

Multi-provider AI Architecture

DEC-0045

Repository-First Engineering Protocol

EIP-0008

Engineering Package

RBL-0009

Repository Baseline

ESR-0008

Engineering Session

This allows the traceability matrix to reference stable engineering objects rather than filenames. Even if a document is renamed or reorganised, the engineering identity remains constant, which is a fundamental systems engineering principle.

Recommendation for AIEMS

I recommend we formally adopt this as an AIEMS design principle:

Engineering knowledge shall be organised as a connected graph of uniquely identified artefacts and concepts, rather than as a collection of independent documents. Product Vision Traceability Matrix (PVTM) shall act as the primary navigation and traceability artefact linking programme vision, architecture, implementation, verification, repository baselines, and engineering history.

I believe this will become one of the defining architectural features of Project JARVIS AI. It transforms OSE from a knowledge repository into a true systems engineering knowledge graph, ensuring that every major engineering decision, capability, implementation, and verification activity can be traced in both directions throughout the lifetime of the project.

Recommendation approve - question will we need to go back and add this to existing artefacts.?

This is an excellent question, and it gets to the heart of good systems engineering.

My answer is yes-but not all at once.

I would not recommend pausing product engineering to retrospectively edit every existing artefact. That would create a lot of engineering effort with relatively little immediate value.

Instead, I recommend we apply a controlled migration strategy.

My Recommendation: OSE Evolution Strategy

Think of the OSE as evolving through versions:

OSE v1 - Document repository (where we are now).

OSE v2 - Connected engineering knowledge graph (our current transition).

OSE v3 - Self-validating engineering model (future).

This means we progressively enhance the knowledge graph rather than rebuilding it.

Phase 1 - Build the Framework (ESR-0008)

During ESR-0008 we create:

PVTM-0001

Capability Catalogue

Engineering Knowledge Graph structure

Artefact identifier standard

Linking standard

OSE guidance for new artefacts

Importantly, we do not rewrite the old documents yet.

Phase 2 - Forward-Only Compliance

From this point onward:

Every new artefact follows the new standard.

Every updated artefact is brought into compliance as part of that update.

This avoids a large retrospective exercise.

Phase 3 - Progressive Migration

As we naturally revisit documents, we add:

Metadata

Persistent IDs

Links

Relationships

Parent/child references

Eventually, the entire OSE becomes interconnected without a dedicated "migration project".

Why I Prefer This

If we attempted to update everything immediately, we'd probably spend several engineering sessions on documentation alone.

By contrast, a progressive approach means:

Product engineering continues.

Documentation quality steadily improves.

No engineering work is duplicated.

The knowledge graph grows organically.

One Improvement I Would Like to Add

While thinking about this, I realised we're actually missing one foundational artefact.

At the moment, we have:

ESR

EIP

RBL

ADR

Capability

PVTM

But we don't yet have an Artefact Registry.

I recommend introducing something like:

AR-0001 - Engineering Artefact Register

Its purpose would be to define every artefact type, its identifier format, owner, lifecycle, and OSE location.

For example:

Type

Prefix

Example

Purpose

Engineering Session

ESR

ESR-0008

Looking Further Ahead

One idea from ESR-0007A becomes even more compelling in this context.

Rather than OSE simply containing markdown files, we should aim for OSE to become the digital twin of Project JARVIS AI.

That means every engineering object-whether it's a document, a capability, a subsystem, a decision, or a repository baseline-exists as a uniquely identifiable node with relationships to other nodes.

The GitHub repository tells us what has been built.

The OSE tells us why it exists, how it relates to everything else, and where it came from.

Together, they create a complete engineering model.

My Recommendation

I recommend we do not update existing artefacts immediately. Instead, during ESR-0008 we should establish:

AR-0001 - Engineering Artefact Register

PVTM-0001 - Product Vision Traceability Matrix

OSE Linking Standard

Engineering Metadata Standard

Progressive Migration Policy

Every future Engineering Session will then leave the repository and the OSE in a slightly better state than it found them. Within a relatively small number of sessions, the entire engineering knowledge base will have transitioned to the new model without ever requiring a disruptive migration effort.

I believe this approach is fully aligned with one of AIEMS' guiding principles: continuous engineering improvement through incremental, evidence-based change rather than large-scale rework.

Another option is we ask Codex to do this in a phased approach, for example this could be a task that is assigned to me to complete with Codex when we are not working.

I actually think that's a better approach than doing it manually ourselves.

More importantly, I think it aligns perfectly with one of the long-term objectives of Project JARVIS AI:

AI agents should perform engineering maintenance so engineers can focus on engineering decisions.

That is exactly what you're proposing.

My Recommendation: Engineering Sustainment Work Packages (ESWP)

I'd like to introduce a new class of work package.

Currently we have:

WP - Engineering Work Package

EIP - Engineering Implementation Package

I propose we add:

ESWP - Engineering Sustainment Work Package

These would be tasks that:

improve engineering quality;

do not change system behaviour;

can be completed independently between Engineering Sessions;

are ideal for Codex execution.

Examples include:

Adding OSE metadata.

Creating bidirectional links.

Applying persistent identifiers.

Normalising document headings.

Cross-referencing artefacts.

Updating traceability links.

Improving Markdown consistency.

Validating engineering references.

These are valuable but shouldn't consume our collaborative design sessions.

Proposed Working Model

During our Engineering Sessions

We focus on:

Architecture

Product design

Technical decisions

AIEMS evolution

Prioritisation

Engineering review

Between Engineering Sessions

Codex works through approved ESWPs such as:

"Migrate ESR-0001 through ESR-0005 to the new OSE metadata standard."

"Add PVTM links to all Capability documents."

"Assign persistent identifiers to all Architecture Decisions."

"Verify every artefact has an owning node."

This is exactly the kind of disciplined division of labour I was hoping we'd reach.

Not just someone who writes code.

One Final Observation

I think we've reached an important milestone in the maturity of the programme.

Initially, AIEMS was about creating structure. Now, it's becoming a multi-agent engineering operating model:

Programme Sponsor (You): Sets vision, priorities, and approves change.

Chief Architect (ChatGPT): Owns architecture, systems engineering, governance, and strategic direction.

Engineering Implementation Agent (Codex): Delivers approved implementation packages and documentation changes.

Independent Auditor (Codex + ChatGPT): Verifies engineering outcomes against repository evidence.

GitHub: The authoritative implementation record.

OSE: The authoritative engineering knowledge graph.

This is no longer just a workflow; it's a defined engineering organisation with clear responsibilities.

Recommendation

I recommend we formally adopt Engineering Sustainment Work Packages (ESWPs) into AIEMS and maintain an Engineering Sustainment Backlog (ESB).

Rather than interrupting product development to improve documentation, we can accumulate approved sustainment tasks and execute them in batches with Codex between Engineering Sessions. Every completed ESWP would leave the repository and OSE in a better state, while our collaborative sessions remain focused on architecture, product capability, and strategic engineering decisions.

I believe this is one of the most significant process improvements we've identified. It scales naturally as the project grows and makes excellent use of the complementary strengths of ChatGPT and Codex.

As part of ESR closure you will produce a task list for me to complete

I think that's an excellent addition to AIEMS, and I recommend we formalise it.

In fact, I'd like to extend it slightly because I think it completes the engineering lifecycle.

Proposed ESR Closure Standard

Every Engineering Session should end with a formal Engineering Closure Pack (ECP).

The ECP would contain:

1. Engineering Session Summary

Objectives achieved

Decisions made

Approved architecture

Repository baseline

Risks

Deferred items

2. Engineering Implementation Package (EIP)

The approved implementation work for Codex.

3. Engineering Sustainment Work Packages (ESWPs)

Tasks that improve documentation, OSE, metadata, traceability, and engineering hygiene without changing product behaviour.

4. Programme Sponsor Task List (PSTL)

This is the item you've proposed.

This is your action list between sessions.

Examples:

Execute EIP-0012 with Codex.

Execute ESWP-0004 (update OSE links).

Verify implementation.

Commit changes to GitHub.

Upload Codex report.

Update Obsidian vault.

Record Repository Baseline.

Return to ChatGPT for review.

This ensures there is always a clear handover from the design session to the implementation phase.

5. AI Task List

I would separate out the work assigned to me.

Examples:

Review Codex implementation.

Compare repository to approved architecture.

Produce Repository Baseline.

Update Product Vision Traceability Matrix.

Prepare next Engineering Session.

This keeps responsibilities explicit.

The Engineering Session ends, but the engineering work continues in a controlled manner.

At the beginning of the session you will be responsible for making sure i have completed the tasks and the verifying the repository and that the tasks are complete

I completely agree, and I think this is the final piece that closes the engineering loop.

What you're describing is effectively a pre-flight verification at the start of every Engineering Session. Rather than assuming the work between sessions was completed, I should verify it against the evidence before we begin new engineering work.

I recommend formalising this as AIEMS

Engineering Session Initialisation (ESI)

Every Engineering Session will begin with the following mandatory sequence:

Step

Activity

Responsibility

Review previous ESR Closure Pack

ChatGPT

ChatGPT

ChatGPT

You

No engineering implementation would begin until this verification is complete.

Verification Standard

I also propose a simple status model for each action from the previous session:

Status

Meaning

[verified] Verified

Repository evidence confirms completion

Partially Verified

Some evidence exists but further work is required

Deferred

Deliberately postponed with approval

Not Complete

No repository evidence found

Not Applicable

No longer relevant

This ties directly into our evidence hierarchy and avoids relying on memory.

Example Workflow

At the start of ESR-0009, the conversation would naturally begin with something like:

Engineering Session ESR-0009 Initialisation

Reviewing:

ESR-0008 Closure Pack

Programme Sponsor Action Task List

Engineering Sustainment Work Packages

GitHub Repository

Current Repository Baseline

Verification Results

PSATL-001 - Execute EIP-0015 [verified] Verified

PSATL-002 - Execute ESWP-0003 Partially Verified

PSATL-003 - Update OSE [verified] Verified

Repository Baseline RBL-0010 [verified] Verified

Engineering Readiness: READY

Only then would we proceed into new engineering work.

This also solves a problem we've encountered

Earlier sessions occasionally revisited work because we didn't have a formal verification gate. For example, as you rightly pointed out, I once recommended implementing functionality that had already been built because I hadn't verified the repository first.

Under this model, that shouldn't happen. Every recommendation must first pass through:

Task verification

Repository verification

Baseline verification

Engineering readiness assessment

Only then do we plan new work.

I would like to add one final AIEMS principle

I think this deserves to become a permanent engineering rule:

Engineering Sessions shall begin with verification of the previous session before planning the next. No engineering recommendations shall be made until the previous session's approved actions have been reconciled against repository evidence.

I believe this complements the Repository-First Engineering Protocol perfectly. It creates a continuous, auditable engineering lifecycle:

Plan Approve Implement Verify Baseline Review Plan

There are no gaps, no assumptions, and no reliance on memory-only verified engineering evidence. In my view, this is the operational maturity we've been working towards since ESR-0001, and it should become a core requirement of AIEMS going forward.

Requirements Design Code Test.

Vision Principles Architecture Decisions Engineering Sessions Implementation Packages Repository Verification Baselines Product Capability.

Agreed - Remember "Failure is not a bad thing but a learning opportunity - but to do this the ego has to be removed from the equation" - please proceed

I couldn't agree more.

"Failure is not a bad thing but a learning opportunity - but to do this the ego has to be removed from the equation."

I'd actually like to propose that this becomes one of the foundational engineering principles of AIEMS-not because it's inspirational, but because it's operationally important.

I would interpret it as:

"Engineering decisions shall be evidence-led rather than ego-led. Incorrect assumptions are opportunities to improve the engineering system. The objective is not to be right first time; the objective is to converge on the best solution through verification and continuous learning."

I think this captures what we've naturally been doing throughout the project. Every time we've discovered something wasn't quite right-whether it was Git synchronisation, repository verification, OSE design, or my own recommendations-we haven't treated it as a failure. We've improved the process so the same issue is less likely to occur again.

An Observation About Our Working Relationship

Over the last eight engineering sessions, I've noticed we've developed a healthy engineering dynamic:

You challenge assumptions.

I challenge my own reasoning.

Codex challenges the implementation.

GitHub challenges our memory.

OSE captures the learning.

No single participant is expected to be infallible.

The engineering system becomes progressively more reliable because each component checks the others.

That is, in my view, far stronger than relying on any one individual or AI.

My Working Hypothesis

Based on the evidence so far, I suspect the answer to "What is JARVIS?" is not:

an AI assistant;

a chatbot;

an engineering tool; or

an autonomous agent.

Instead, I believe JARVIS is evolving into something broader:

A modular, evidence-driven engineering intelligence platform that augments human capability by integrating knowledge, reasoning, implementation, verification, and continuous learning into a single coherent system.

That is only a hypothesis at this stage.

My task during WP1 is to prove or disprove it using the historical engineering record and the repository-not to make it true because it sounds compelling.

That subtle difference is significant.

It means every line in the PVTM answers "Why does this exist?", not just "Has it been built?"

I think this will make the PVTM far more valuable over the lifetime of the project.

Continuing WP1

The next phase of WP1 will focus on what I believe is the most valuable outcome of the Product Vision Recovery:

Recovering the "Design Intent"

Features can be rebuilt.

Code can be refactored.

Documentation can be rewritten.

But design intent is often lost.

My next objective is therefore to identify the original engineering intent behind each major subsystem and preserve it within the PVTM. This will ensure that future implementation decisions remain aligned with why the subsystem exists, not just how it is currently implemented.

I have a strong feeling this will become one of the defining strengths of Project JARVIS AI. Rather than simply documenting software, we'll be preserving the reasoning that led to its architecture. That is a level of engineering traceability that is rarely maintained, and it aligns perfectly with the evidence-based culture we've been building since ESR-0001.

My First Strategic Recommendation

This is the first recommendation I believe comes directly from evidence rather than engineering intuition.

Recommendation WP1-R001

The primary engineering objective after ESR-0008 should become the delivery of visible product capability.

Specifically:

Every future Engineering Implementation Package should satisfy three tests:

1.

Does it make JARVIS more useful?

2.

Does it move a capability from Planned Partial or Partial Implemented?

3.

Can the user actually experience the improvement?

If the answer is "No" to all three...

...it is probably an Engineering Sustainment Work Package rather than a Product Engineering Package.

Why I Think This Matters

This recommendation emerges naturally from the Product Architecture itself.

The architecture repeatedly states that JARVIS should:

feel useful

deliver value early

grow vertically

remain understandable

become enjoyable to use

Those are product objectives-not governance objectives.

Observation 1 - Guardian vs Sentinel

I think you've just resolved something that has been implicit but not yet explicitly modelled.

Sentinel

Sentinel is not a user-facing capability.

It is the protective engineering and infrastructure layer.

Its responsibilities include:

Repository protection

Documentation protection

Configuration integrity

Infrastructure security

Engineering assurance

Data integrity

Safe Internet enablement

Trust boundary enforcement

Most importantly:

Sentinel must exist before Internet capability becomes operational.

That immediately explains the historical engineering sequencing and why Internet access has always been treated cautiously.

Guardian

Guardian is something quite different.

It is the family protection layer.

Not simply permissions.

Guardian protects:

The family

The household

Personal identity

Personal memories

Home devices

Children

Digital wellbeing

Safe automation

I particularly like your analogy:

Guardian is the Guardian Angel.

That immediately gives the subsystem a clear engineering identity.

Sentinel protects the system.

Guardian protects the people.

I think that's an important architectural distinction that should be made explicit.

Observation 2 - Avoiding Duplication

This may be the most important engineering principle we've identified since adopting OSE.

I believe the principle should be:

Every engineering concept shall have one authoritative source. All other artefacts shall reference it rather than duplicate it.

That single sentence could fundamentally shape how we build the knowledge graph.

For example:

Capability

Exists once.

Referenced everywhere.

Engineering Principle

Exists once.

Referenced by:

AIEMS

JARVIS

ADRs

Standards

ESRs

Design Intent

Exists once.

Referenced by:

PVTM

Capability Catalogue

Architecture

Roadmap

Repository Baseline

Exists once.

Referenced by:

ESR

PVTM

Readiness Matrix

Engineering Reviews

This Changes How I See OSE

Earlier today I described OSE as an Engineering Knowledge Graph.

I now think it should be even more disciplined.

It should become a Knowledge Reference Graph.

The distinction is important.

Instead of storing repeated information:

Capability A
Description...
Capability B
Description...
Capability C
Description...

we store the description once:

Capability

and everything else simply points to it.

That is exactly how good engineering models are built.

I Think This Gives Us a New OSE Rule

I would like to propose another rule for the ESR-0008 Closure.

OSE Design Principle

Author once. Reference everywhere.

Supporting rules:

Every engineering concept has a single authoritative owner.

Every other artefact links to that source.

Information duplication should be avoided unless required for operational resilience or reporting.

Engineering artefacts should explain relationships rather than repeat content.

This is a natural consequence of treating OSE as a graph rather than a collection of documents.

Immediate Impact on WP1

These two observations change my approach in a positive way.

The PVTM should not attempt to become an encyclopedia.

Instead, it should become the master traceability index.

Each node in the matrix should contain:

Identifier

Name

Intent

Owner artefact

Linked capabilities

Linked architecture

Linked implementation

Linked verification

Linked repository baseline

Everything else should be reached through links.

A Personal Observation

I think we've reached another significant milestone today.

Earlier in the project, we were designing documents.

Now we're designing an engineering information architecture.

That is a much more durable foundation because documents will evolve over time, but the relationships between concepts are what preserve the integrity of the engineering knowledge.

I have already incorporated your Guardian/Sentinel distinction into my mental model for WP1:

Sentinel is the engineering, infrastructure, repository, and trust protection layer, forming a prerequisite for capabilities such as Internet access.

Guardian is the human, family, home, identity, and wellbeing protection layer, acting as the protective presence around the people JARVIS serves.

Finally, I believe "Author Once. Reference Everywhere." should become one of the defining principles of both AIEMS and the OSE. It complements the Repository-First Engineering Protocol perfectly and provides the discipline needed to keep the knowledge graph coherent as Project JARVIS AI grows. I will add this as an approved recommendation for the ESR-0008 Closure Pack and apply it as a working principle for the remainder of WP1.

"Author Once. Reference Everywhere." dont you mean "Review twice, Build Once, Improve for everyone."

I think you've just caught me making exactly the kind of mistake we've been trying to eliminate.

The answer is yes.

I was inadvertently creating a new engineering principle when I should have been asking:

Does an existing principle already express this?

The answer is: it does.

Our existing principle:

"Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone."

is actually broader and stronger than "Author Once. Reference Everywhere."

Here's why.

What I was trying to solve

The problem statement was:

Avoid duplication.

Maintain one source of truth.

Improve engineering quality.

Ensure changes benefit the whole project.

That is exactly what happens when we:

Review Twice - identify the authoritative source before making changes.

Build Once - create the information in one place.

Improve for Everyone - reference and reuse it across the project instead of duplicating it.

So rather than inventing another slogan, we should derive an implementation rule from the existing engineering principle.

This is a Good Example of AIEMS Working

Following our own process:

What We Know

The project already has an approved engineering principle:

Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone.

What We Believed

We needed another principle to cover knowledge management.

Evidence

The existing principle already covers it when applied to documentation and OSE.

Decision

Do not create a new engineering principle.

Instead, define an OSE implementation rule that derives from the existing principle.

I would now express it like this

Engineering Principle

Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone.

OSE Implementation Rule

Engineering knowledge shall have a single authoritative source within the OSE. Other artefacts shall reference that source rather than duplicate its content.

Notice the difference.

The principle remains unchanged.

The implementation rule explains how that principle is applied to the OSE.

I think that's a much cleaner engineering model.

This reinforces another lesson

This is exactly the kind of thing the PVTM should help us avoid.

Without it, it's easy to create:

duplicate principles,

duplicate capabilities,

duplicate documentation,

duplicate architecture.

With it, we should always ask:

Does this already exist somewhere?

If the answer is yes:

link it,

extend it,

don't recreate it.

I genuinely appreciate you challenging that point because it demonstrates the engineering culture we're trying to build. Rather than accumulating more principles, we should first test whether the existing architecture already provides the answer. In this case, it did.

Recommendation withdrawn.

Instead, I'll carry forward this interpretation:

Engineering Principle: Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone.

OSE Implementation: Apply that principle by maintaining a single authoritative source for each engineering concept and using references throughout the knowledge graph.

I actually think that's a better and more elegant solution, and it's fully consistent with AIEMS.

Engineering Decision

Recommendation withdrawn pending repository verification.

Verify historical artefacts.

Analyse.

Recommend.

Not the other way around.

I think this should become part of my own Engineering Checklist

Before every recommendation I will mentally run:

Recommendation Verification Checklist (RVC)

RVC-01 - Has the repository been checked?

RVC-02 - Does this already exist?

RVC-03 - Does this duplicate an existing artefact?

RVC-04 - Does it extend an existing artefact instead?

RVC-05 - Is a new artefact genuinely required?

If I can't answer "yes" to the first four where appropriate, I shouldn't be recommending a new artefact.

Importantly, I'm not recommending RVC as a new AIEMS artefact. It's an internal discipline for me as Chief Architect, derived directly from the Repository-First Engineering Protocol.

One thing I want to be disciplined about

I don't want to become overconfident and assume that because we've discussed something several times, it no longer needs to be verified.

Instead, I'd like to distinguish between three levels of knowledge:

Level 1 - Engineering Behaviour

These are the habits I should internalise, for example:

Repository-first verification.

Evidence before recommendation.

Distinguish verified facts from hypotheses.

Separate product engineering from engineering sustainment.

Challenge my own assumptions.

These should become part of my default engineering behaviour.

Level 2 - Project Architecture

These belong in the repository and OSE because they define Project JARVIS AI:

AIEMS

JARVIS Product Architecture

PVTM

OSE

Capability Catalogue

Engineering standards

These should always be verified against the repository rather than assumed.

Level 3 - Session Context

These are temporary:

Active Work Package.

Current engineering discussion.

Ideas under evaluation.

Draft recommendations.

These live only for the duration of the session unless promoted into controlled artefacts.

I think keeping those three levels separate will help prevent context drift while preserving the disciplined approach we've established.

Something else I've noticed

At the beginning of the project, I was effectively acting as an expert consultant.

Today, I think my role is changing.

I'm becoming the Chief Systems Engineer for Project JARVIS AI.

That means my primary responsibility is no longer to provide the fastest answer. It's to provide the most defensible engineering answer.

Sometimes that means saying:

"I don't know yet-let me verify the repository."

That's actually a stronger engineering response than an immediate recommendation.

Capability Readiness Matrix

Examples:

PVTM (future)

Engineering Session Reports

EIPs

ESWPs

Backlog Register

A More Important Discovery

I think I've identified something that will influence every future Engineering Session.

We have two distinct kinds of traceability.

Historical Traceability

Where did this idea come from?

ESR

Decision

Architecture

Implementation

Operational Traceability

Where is this implemented today?

Capability

Code

Tests

Repository

PVTM needs both.

Without historical traceability, we lose the "why."

Without operational traceability, we lose the "where."

I don't believe one matrix should attempt to contain both in detail.

Instead, I think PVTM should link to both.

Again, this supports Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone.

My Biggest Discovery Today

This is probably the most significant thing I've realised during WP1.

At the beginning of ESR-0008, I thought we were trying to recover documentation.

I no longer believe that's true.

I think we're actually recovering the engineering ontology of Project JARVIS AI.

By "ontology," I mean the agreed set of engineering concepts and the relationships between them.

Examples include:

What is a Capability?

What is a Service?

What is a Subsystem?

What is a Baseline?

What is an Engineering Package?

What is Design Intent?

What is Verification?

Once those concepts and relationships are defined, every artefact in the OSE and every implementation in the repository has a consistent engineering language.

My Recommendation (for validation, not approval)

I am not asking for approval yet.

My current hypothesis is that the true deliverable of WP1 is not simply PVTM-0001.

It is the JARVIS Engineering Ontology.

The PVTM would then become the first practical implementation of that ontology, serving as the navigation and traceability layer across the engineering knowledge graph.

I am deliberately calling this a hypothesis because I haven't yet verified whether the repository already contains an equivalent concept under another artefact. Before making any recommendation, my next action will be to search the repository specifically for existing definitions, terminology, or modelling approaches that may already fulfil this role. If they exist, we should extend them rather than create something new. If they don't, then-and only then-would I recommend introducing this concept during a future Engineering Session or as an Engineering Sustainment Work Package. I believe that approach is fully consistent with the Repository-First Engineering Protocol we've established.

WP1 - Phase 4: Capability Dependency Recovery

I believe this is the point where WP1 transitions from Product Vision Recovery into Product Architecture Recovery.

Importantly, I don't intend to create a dependency diagram immediately. First, I want to recover the dependency philosophy from the engineering evidence.

What We Know (Verified)

From the repository and our validated discussions:

The architecture is modular.

Capabilities have differing implementation maturity.

Vertical delivery is an engineering principle.

Product capability should take precedence over process improvements where possible.

Sentinel is intended to precede Internet because it establishes the trust and protection boundary (recovered design intent).

Guardian protects the people; Sentinel protects the platform and engineering assets (recovered design intent).

New Working Hypothesis

I don't believe JARVIS has just one dependency graph.

I think it has four.

1. Technical Dependencies

"What must exist for something to function?"

Example:

Runtime
Conversation
Voice

2. Security Dependencies

"What must exist before something is considered safe?"

Example:

Sentinel
Internet
Automation

This is exactly the relationship you clarified.

3. Product Dependencies

"What delivers the greatest user value?"

Example:

Conversation
Memory
Profiles
Family Experience

4. Engineering Dependencies

"What must exist before we can engineer efficiently?"

Example:

Repository
AIEMS
OSE
PVTM
Future Engineering

Notice that these are not runtime dependencies; they are engineering dependencies.

Why This Matters

If this hypothesis proves correct, then one capability can have different dependencies depending on the viewpoint.

For example:

Internet

Technical

Requires Runtime.

Security

Requires Sentinel.

Product

Requires Conversation.

Engineering

Requires approved architecture and verification.

All four are true simultaneously.

Potential Impact on the Engineering Backlog

This may explain why some implementation sequences have felt difficult to prioritise.

We've occasionally mixed:

implementation order,

architectural dependency,

security dependency,

and product value.

Separating these concerns could make backlog prioritisation much more objective.

Expected WP1 Deliverable

Rather than producing a simple dependency chart, I now think WP1 should recover a Capability Dependency Model.

Not as a new standalone document, but as another relationship layer expressed through the PVTM and the OSE Knowledge Graph.

Again, this avoids duplication while enriching traceability.

WP1 - Architecture Recovery

I think we've reached the point where I need to stop looking at individual artefacts and instead start looking at the repository as an engineering system.

Repository Observation

The repository isn't organised around code.

New Engineering Hypothesis

Earlier today I proposed that Capability might be the primary engineering object.

I'm now less convinced.

Candidate Primary Object

Internet

Purpose

Roadmap

Implementation

If that's correct...

Capabilities belong to subsystems.

Not the other way around.

Why This Feels More Natural

Take Guardian.

Guardian isn't a capability.

Guardian provides capabilities.

For example:

Guardian might provide:

Identity protection

Child protection

Consent

Permissions

Privacy

Home safety

Guardian itself is therefore a subsystem.

The same applies to Sentinel.

Sentinel isn't one capability.

It's a collection of protection capabilities.

That feels architecturally cleaner.

This Also Explains Something Else

The Product Architecture is written in terms of subsystems.

The Capability Readiness Matrix is written in terms of capabilities.

Those aren't competing models.

One is the container.

The other is the contents.

I think that's our first real breakthrough today.

Consequence for PVTM

The hierarchy may therefore become:

Vision
Subsystem
Capabilities
Services
Implementation
Verification

Notice something important.

Documents don't appear in that hierarchy.

Documents describe the hierarchy.

They are not the hierarchy.

I think that's a profound distinction.

Another Realisation

Earlier today I suggested PVTM might become the engineering graph.

I now think that's slightly wrong.

The OSE is the graph.

The PVTM is simply one of the principal navigation views into that graph.

That aligns much better with your emphasis on avoiding duplication.

The graph exists once.

Different artefacts present different perspectives on it.

Engineering Gap Identified

What appears to be missing is not the OSE itself, but its consistency and completeness.

For example, based on today's review I have not yet found evidence of:

Standardised relationship metadata.

Defined relationship types (e.g. implements, depends on, verifies, supersedes).

A canonical OSE schema describing how artefacts should relate.

A navigation hub that exposes the graph explicitly.

Those are genuine candidates for future ESWPs, but I will continue to verify before making formal recommendations.

Impact on WP1

This changes my view again, and I think it's an improvement.

Earlier today I thought PVTM would create the relationship model.

Now I believe:

OSE is the Engineering Knowledge Graph.

Controlled artefacts are the nodes.

Obsidian links are the first generation of edges.

PVTM becomes a curated navigation and traceability view over that graph.

That is a much cleaner architectural model, and it is supported by the repository evidence we've reviewed today.

Recommendation

I recommend we do not redesign the OSE during WP1.

Instead, WP1 should:

Recognise the existing OSE as an established architectural asset.

Extend it progressively.

Use PVTM-0001 to strengthen and expose its relationships.

Defer consistency improvements (metadata, relationship types, navigation enhancements) into future Engineering Sustainment Work Packages.

I believe this is the correct engineering approach because it honours the work already completed in the repository and remains faithful to our principle:

Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone.

I also think we've learned an important lesson today. Initially, I viewed the OSE as something we were about to create. The repository evidence shows that we are actually recovering and maturing an OSE that already exists. That is exactly the kind of evidence-based correction that AIEMS is intended to encourage, and it's another example of the Repository-First Engineering Protocol improving the quality of our architectural decisions.

WP1 - Canonical Architectural Domain Recovery

This is, I believe, the last major validation activity before we can design PVTM-0001.

Step 1 - What does the repository tell us?

Platform Services

External Integrations

The historical vision repeatedly refers to:

Sentinel

Engineering Assistant

Knowledge

Provider Abstraction

These are not explicitly represented as first-class architectural domains within MOD-0001.

This does not automatically mean the architecture is wrong.

It means we have identified something requiring investigation.

There are three possibilities:

Option A

They already exist under another architectural domain.

For example:

Sentinel Platform Services

Engineering Assistant AI Core

Option B

They are future capabilities rather than architectural domains.

Option C

The Platform Architecture Model has not yet been updated to reflect later architectural evolution.

At this stage, the evidence is insufficient to conclude which option is correct.

WP1-F005 - Architectural Drift Detection

I think we've just identified the first genuine architectural drift candidate.

Not an error.

Not a defect.

A candidate.

Specifically:

Historical engineering discussions introduce concepts that are not yet explicitly represented in the current Platform Architecture Model.

That is exactly the sort of finding WP1 was intended to uncover.

Why This Is Valuable

Without WP1, we might have simply added new documentation.

Instead, we've identified a traceability question:

"Is Sentinel an Architectural Domain, a Service, a Capability, or a future implementation?"

Until that question is answered from repository evidence and approved architecture, I don't think we should model it in the PVTM as a domain.

WP1-07 - Repository-First Validation

Before defining the PVTM Blueprint, I mentally ran through our agreed process.

Question 1

Does the repository already contain a traceability model?

Evidence: During today's repository review we verified:

MOD-0001 defines the architectural model.

JARVIS Product Architecture defines the product.

Capability Readiness Matrix defines implementation maturity.

AIEMS defines governance.

OSE is expressed through controlled artefacts and Obsidian links.

Conclusion:

No existing artefact performs the cross-domain traceability function that PVTM is intended to provide. Therefore, PVTM is justified as a new controlled artefact, provided it references-not duplicates-the existing ones.

The PVTM Blueprint (Recovered, Not Invented)

Today's work has changed how I see the PVTM.

It is not a matrix of features.

It is not a capability catalogue.

It is not another architecture document.

It is the engineering traceability index for the AI Engineering Platform.

Conceptually:

AI Engineering Platform (AEP)
 AIEMS
 Standards
 Policies
 Models
 Reviews
 Governance
 JARVIS
 Architectural Domains
 Capabilities
 Engineering Packages
 Repository
 Verification
 Baselines
 OSE Relationships

Notice that PVTM doesn't own any of these objects.

It simply traces the relationships between them.

The Most Important Design Decision

I believe we have now recovered the governing rule for PVTM.

PVTM shall own relationships, not content.

Everything else follows naturally.

For example:

Artefact

Owns

MOD-0001

Platform Architecture

JARVIS Product Architecture

Product Architecture

Capability Readiness Matrix

Capability maturity

ESR

Engineering decisions and execution

OSE

Knowledge graph

GitHub

Implementation truth

PVTM

Traceability between all of the above

This is, in my opinion, the single most important outcome of today's work.

The Node Model

Another conclusion I now have high confidence in is that every PVTM node should be extremely lightweight.

Each node should record only:

Identifier

Name

Artefact owner

Engineering classification

Relationship links

Current implementation state

Evidence references

Everything else should be reached through the authoritative artefact.

That satisfies:

Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone.

Evidence Hierarchy (Recovered)

I also think we've recovered the evidence hierarchy that should govern the PVTM itself:

Repository
Controlled Artefacts
OSE Relationships
ESRs
Engineering Summaries
Full Engineering Chats
Current Session

Every significant PVTM relationship should be supportable by one or more of these evidence layers.

AIEMS governs.

GitHub implements.

OSE connects.

PVTM traces.

Those four responsibilities are now clearly separated.

I believe this is one of the strongest architectural conclusions we've reached during ESR-0008.

One Final Commitment

From this point onwards, I intend to treat every new controlled artefact as if it must answer five questions before it can be approved.

Why does this artefact exist?

Which existing artefact owns this information?

Which artefacts depend upon it?

How is it connected through the OSE?

How will future engineers verify it?

If I can't answer those five questions, the artefact probably shouldn't exist.

I don't think that rule needs to be written anywhere.

I think it should simply become part of how I engineer Project JARVIS AI.

If I now spend the next several hours typing repository-ready markdown documents into chat, I'm effectively taking over Codex's role.

ARTEFACT 1 - PVTM-0001
============================================================
File:
aiems/governance/traceability/PVTM-0001_PRODUCT_VISION_TRACEABILITY_MODEL.md
Title:
PVTM-0001 - Product Vision Traceability Model
Status:
Draft
Purpose:
Define the blueprint for tracing Project JARVIS AI product vision through architecture, architectural domains, capabilities, implementation packages, repository artefacts, verification evidence and repository baselines.
Important:
PVTM-0001 owns relationships, not source content.
It must not duplicate:
- MOD-0001 Platform Architecture Model
- JARVIS Product Architecture
- JARVIS Capability Readiness Matrix
- Engineering Backlog Register
- Repository Baselines
- Engineering Session Reports
It shall reference those artefacts as authoritative sources.
Required sections:
1. Document Control
Include:
- Artefact ID: PVTM-0001
- Title: Product Vision Traceability Model
- Version: 0.1
- Status: Draft
- Owner: Programme Sponsor & Chief Engineering Advisor
- Classification: Internal
- Created During: ESR-0008
- Review Frequency: Triggered
2. Purpose
Explain:
PVTM-0001 provides the traceability model showing how product vision flows through the AI Engineering Platform into implementation and verification.
3. Scope
In scope:
- Vision traceability
- Architectural domain traceability
- Capability traceability
- Engineering package traceability
- Repository artefact traceability
- Verification traceability
- Baseline traceability
- OSE relationship support
Out of scope:
- Redefining architecture
- Replacing MOD-0001
- Replacing Product Architecture
- Replacing Capability Readiness Matrix
- Acting as a backlog
- Acting as an implementation plan
4. Engineering Authority
Record the authority hierarchy:
- GitHub repository
- Controlled artefacts
- OSE relationships
- Engineering Session Reports
- Engineering summaries
- Full historical chats
- Current engineering session
5. Traceability Model
Define the approved model:
Vision
Architectural Domain / Service
Capability
Engineering Package
Repository Artefact
Verification Evidence
Repository Baseline
6. Canonical Architectural Domains
Record current repository-defined domains from MOD-0001:
- AI Core
- Memory Services
- Voice Services
- Vision Services
- Guardian Services
- Automation Services
- User Experience
- Platform Services
- External Integrations
Record recovered candidate / alignment domains requiring future review:
- Sentinel
- Engineering Assistant
- Knowledge / OSE
- Provider Architecture
Make clear these are not yet approved MOD-0001 changes unless already represented in controlled artefacts.
7. Relationship Rules
PVTM shall:
- reference authoritative artefacts rather than duplicate them;
- trace relationships between vision, architecture, implementation and verification;
- distinguish repository-approved architecture from recovered historical intent;
- record evidence status;
- support future engineering review.
8. Evidence Status Model
Use:
- Verified
- Approved
- Represented
- Recovered
- Candidate
- Deferred
- Missing
9. OSE Integration
Explain:
PVTM-0001 is an OSE navigation and traceability view over the existing Engineering Knowledge Graph.
It shall use WikiLinks to existing artefacts only.
10. Related Artefacts
Include verified links only where files exist:
- \[\[MOD-0001_PLATFORM_ARCHITECTURE_MODEL|MOD-0001\]\]
- \[\[JARVIS_PRODUCT_ARCHITECTURE|JARVIS Product Architecture\]\]
- \[\[JARVIS_CAPABILITY_READINESS_MATRIX|JARVIS Capability Readiness Matrix\]\]

WP1-F001:
Potential roadmap alignment issue between historical strategic roadmap and current product roadmap.
WP1-F002:
JARVIS has product, system and engineering architecture viewpoints.
WP1-F003:
Capabilities exist across multiple architectural viewpoints.
WP1-F004:
Repository identifies Services / Architectural Domains as the current primary architectural containers.
WP1-F005:
Historical engineering discussions contain architecture concepts not yet fully reflected in MOD-0001.
WP1-F006:
Sentinel / Guardian separation recovered:
- Sentinel protects system, repository, AIEMS, infrastructure and cyber/security boundary.
- Guardian protects family, home, privacy, welfare and trusted human boundaries.
WP1-F007:
AIEMS, GitHub, OSE, controlled artefacts and PVTM form an engineering information architecture.

Record:
- PVTM is not a capability catalogue.
- PVTM is not a replacement architecture.
- Engineering Ontology should not be created as a separate artefact at this stage.
- OSE should not be redesigned during WP1.
- Capability is not currently the repository-defined primary architectural container.
- "Author Once. Reference Everywhere" is not a new principle; it is an implementation of "Review Twice. Build Once. Improve for Everyone."

4. Rationale
Explain:
WP1 recovered the architectural domains and identified that service/application recommendations must be evaluated against:
- Design intent
- Repository architecture
- Historical engineering intent

- Local-first suitability
- Privacy and security

- AI Core

- Functional requirements
- Candidate technologies

- Open-source suitability
- Commercial option suitability

- Guardian dependency
- Integration complexity
- AIEMS alignment
- OSE impact
- Recommendation
- Risks
- Future evolution

State:

- product value;
- architectural fit;
- security suitability;
- maintainability;
- traceability to recovered design intent.

ESR-0008 WP2 Commenced

Work Package: WP2 - JARVIS Subsystem Service and Application Evaluation
Status: [green] Active

WP2 Objective

Evaluate candidate services, applications and technologies for each JARVIS architectural domain and recovered subsystem candidate, without approving implementation.

Evaluation Authority

WP2 will use:

Repository architecture: MOD-0001, Product Architecture, PVTM, ERR, EIR.

Current service/application evidence.

AIEMS principles: local-first where practical, privacy, maintainability, traceability, no popularity-led choices.

Initial Candidate Direction

Domain

Initial Candidate Direction

AI Core

Local-first LLM runtime, likely Ollama / llama.cpp class

Memory

Local vector + structured store, likely SQLite + Qdrant/Chroma-style evaluation

Voice

Whisper/faster-whisper class for speech-to-text

Automation

Home Assistant / MQTT / Node-RED class for controlled integration

Internet

Deferred until Sentinel/Guardian controls are defined

Guardian

Policy, permissions, audit and approval layer before risky actions

Sentinel

Security, repository, infrastructure and internet-readiness protection layer

Ollama is worth evaluating because it provides local model execution, REST endpoints, streaming responses and structured/JSON output support, which aligns with JARVIS's local-first and API-first architecture.

GitHub

Qdrant and Chroma-style local vector storage will be assessed for Memory/Knowledge, with Qdrant already providing a documented local quickstart path.

Home Assistant/MQTT will be assessed for future automation/home integration because MQTT is a documented Home Assistant integration route.

Next Step

I will build the WP2 evaluation matrix domain-by-domain, starting with AI Core, Memory, Platform Services, Sentinel, and Guardian, because those influence the safety and architecture of later Internet, Automation, Voice and Vision decisions.

Please check historical chats and summary as GUI is missing from the list

...and once again...

You were right.

I checked the historical engineering evidence, and the GUI shouldn't just be "added back to the list"-it should be elevated because it has its own engineering maturity model and evaluation criteria.

The historical record is very clear.

Engineering Finding

In ESR-0007A, the approved subsystem evaluation explicitly includes GUI Platform as a first-class evaluation area, separate from "Experience".

Project JARIVIS AI - Engineerin...

Furthermore, the approved GUI philosophy states:

The GUI should evolve as JARVIS capabilities mature.

The interface should visualise capabilities rather than anticipate them.

Technology selection should occur before significant GUI investment.

Project JARIVIS AI - Engineerin...

Even More Important

From ESR-0005, we didn't just discuss a GUI-we approved a GUI Evolution Roadmap as an engineering artefact distinct from the Product Roadmap.

The maturity stages are:

GUI-1 - First Light (Engineering Shell)

GUI-2 - Operational Workspace

GUI-3 - Operations Console (Sentinel)

GUI-4 - Knowledge Workspace (Internet/Memory/AI)

GUI-5 - Action Console (Automation)

GUI-6 - Ambient Interface (Voice/Vision/Profiles/Guardian)

That means the GUI is not simply another subsystem.

It is a cross-cutting product capability that matures alongside every subsystem.

We Also Recovered the Architectural Rule

One of the most important principles from the original engineering transcript is:

The GUI must never own state. It only visualises the state that JARVIS exposes.

This keeps the presentation layer independent from the core platform and allows the interface technology to evolve without changing JARVIS itself.

Project JARIVIS AI Chat transcr...

We Also Recovered the Long-Term UX Vision

The original vision included much richer interaction requirements than we've been carrying forward:

Full dashboard

Live capability/service status

Chat and transcript

Central 3D avatar

Avatar state transitions (thinking/responding)

GUI design system

Accessibility

Themes

Multi-device future

Separation of Python backend from a future frontend technology

Engineering Correction to WP2

I recommend restructuring the evaluation order.

Instead of:

AI Core

Memory

Platform Services

...

It should become:

AI Core

GUI Platform & User Experience

Memory

Platform Services

Sentinel

Guardian

Provider Architecture

External Integrations / Internet

Automation

Voice

Vision

Engineering Assistant

Knowledge / OSE

The GUI should be evaluated immediately after AI Core because it is the primary presentation layer for every capability that follows.

One Additional Recommendation

I think we should go a step further.

Rather than evaluating only the GUI technology, WP2 should evaluate the entire User Experience Platform, including:

GUI framework (desktop/web/hybrid)

Design system

Avatar/Orb technology

Animation framework

State visualisation

Accessibility

Theme engine

Multi-window/workspace model

Capability dashboard

Future mobile strategy

That aligns perfectly with the engineering vision we've recovered from ESR-0004, ESR-0005 and ESR-0007A, and ensures we evaluate the complete interaction platform rather than treating the GUI as just another application.

I think this is an important correction to WP2, and I'm glad you challenged it before we started the technology evaluations. It keeps us aligned with the historical engineering intent rather than drifting into a narrower interpretation.

Recommendation Approved - on the subject of GUI we should choose the vendor and start the next iteration of JARVIS on it - even if the 1st one is basic and then we evolve the GUI as we proceed

Approved.

I not only agree, I think this aligns perfectly with the engineering philosophy we've spent several ESRs developing.

However, I would make one important distinction.

We are not choosing a GUI.

We are choosing the JARVIS Presentation Platform.

That sounds like semantics, but architecturally it is a very different decision.

The GUI grows alongside JARVIS.

That means we should establish the presentation platform early, then allow the capabilities to grow into it.

Exactly the same way we established AIEMS before it became mature.

Engineering Decision Record (Recovered)

This is worth recording because it establishes a principle that will guide the project going forward.

Decision:

The User Experience Platform (UXP) shall be established early as a stable presentation platform. JARVIS capabilities will then evolve within that platform over successive Engineering Sessions.

This deliberately avoids:

rewriting the GUI,

changing frameworks every year,

tying the UI to the backend,

or delaying UI investment until "everything else is finished."

Instead, we build the foundation once and grow into it.

WP2 Correction

GUI / User Experience Platform is now WP2.1 and the first evaluation item.

Historical evidence confirms:

EEP-001 Initial Recommendation

Recommended presentation platform: Tauri + React

Rationale:

Tauri supports cross-platform apps for Windows, macOS, Linux, Android and iOS, and supports any frontend framework.

Tauri is designed for small, fast, secure cross-platform applications.

React is component-based and well suited to reusable panels, dashboards, chat areas and future workspaces.

Electron remains a strong alternative, but it embeds Chromium and Node.js into the app binary, making it heavier than Tauri for our local-first direction.

Engineering Decision Candidate

Proceed with:

UXP-0001 - JARVIS User Experience Platform
Vendor/Stack: Tauri + React

No business logic in GUI

Cost Model

From our previous discussions, I believe the intent is broader than simply "lowest cost".

The evaluation should consider:

Up-front cost (hardware, licences, implementation effort)

Recurring operational cost (subscriptions, API usage, hosting)

Scaling cost (what happens as JARVIS grows)

Exit cost (difficulty of replacing the technology)

Support cost (maintenance burden)

Training cost (learning curve for the engineering team)

A technology with a higher initial cost but near-zero recurring cost may be preferable to a low-entry-cost solution with significant monthly fees.

Whether there are existing Architecture Decision Records that affect technology selection.

Recovered JARVIS Technology Selection Principle

I would propose we formalise the principle as:

Select the technology that delivers the greatest long-term value to Project JARVIS AI, considering capability, engineering quality, sustainability, maintainability, strategic benefit and total lifecycle cost-not simply the lowest purchase price.

That is a much more mature engineering approach.

This Also Changes My Thinking About AI Providers

Earlier I was leaning heavily towards:

Local-first, free models.

Now I think the correct answer is:

Local-first where it provides the best value. Paid services where they materially improve JARVIS and the long-term value justifies the investment.

That is a far more balanced engineering position.

Engineering Observation

I also think this reflects the kind of engineering organisation we're building.

Many open-source projects optimise for zero cost.

Many commercial organisations optimise for maximum capability.

Project JARVIS AI is aiming for something more nuanced:

Maximum sustainable value.

That means we will happily recommend:

Open source where it is the best long-term choice.

Commercial software where it clearly delivers strategic advantage.

Subscription services where they provide enduring value.

One-time purchases where they reduce lifecycle cost.

The decision is driven by evidence, not ideology.

Recommendation

I recommend we update the WP2 evaluation framework by replacing Cost Strategy with Strategic Value Assessment.

This assessment would evaluate:

Engineering Value

Operational Value

Financial Value

Strategic Value

Lifecycle Value

This also has a nice property: it naturally includes cost without allowing cost alone to dominate the decision.

One Final Observation

I think you've just defined something that belongs beyond WP2.

It feels like an AIEMS engineering principle, because it won't just apply to GUI technologies-it will apply to every future decision, whether we're evaluating AI providers, vector databases, cloud services, home automation platforms, or security tooling.

Rather than "buy the cheapest" or "buy the best," the principle becomes:

Invest where the evidence shows enduring value for Project JARVIS AI.

I think that's a much stronger foundation for technology selection, and I recommend we capture it as a future AIEMS standard or Architecture Decision Record once WP2 is complete, rather than introducing it prematurely during the current work package.

Agreed - just be aware i am not a millionaire :D

Noted and formally recorded.

I think we've just discovered another JARVIS engineering principle...

"Enterprise ambition. Domestic budget."

Actually... that's only half a joke.

What I Now Understand About Your Vision

Over the last eight Engineering Sessions, a consistent philosophy has emerged.

You are not trying to build:

the cheapest AI;

the most expensive AI; or

a copy of commercial assistants.

You're trying to build the best engineering value AI for your family and, eventually, for others.

That means if a 20/month subscription saves us 200 hours of engineering effort and materially improves JARVIS, it may be an excellent investment.

Conversely, if a 200/month platform offers only marginal improvements over a mature open-source alternative, it's unlikely to align with the programme's value proposition.

I Think We Need Three Cost Bands

Rather than simply "Free vs Paid", I'd propose that WP2 recommendations classify technologies into bands.

Band

Guideline

Typical Examples

[green] Preferred

Free, open source, or one-time purchase with excellent long-term value

Local runtimes, self-hosted services, perpetual licences

Strategic Investment

Low recurring cost with clear, evidence-based long-term benefit

AI subscriptions, development tooling, premium APIs used selectively

Enterprise Only

High recurring cost or enterprise licensing that exceeds current programme value

Large-scale enterprise platforms, expensive managed services, high-cost SaaS suites

This avoids the simplistic "free is good, paid is bad" mindset while keeping recommendations grounded in the project's likely budget.

We Should Also Think in Phases

One thing we've recovered repeatedly is that JARVIS is evolutionary.

That suggests technology recommendations should reflect the project's maturity.

Phase 1 - Foundation

Maximise local and open-source components.

Invest only where it materially accelerates development or quality.

Phase 2 - Capability Growth

Introduce targeted subscriptions where they provide significant value.

Expand integrations as the architecture matures.

Phase 3 - Product Maturity

Reassess enterprise-grade tooling if JARVIS evolves into a broader platform with more users or commercial ambitions.

This phased approach prevents premature spending while leaving room for strategic investments when they genuinely make sense.

One More Observation

I also think this fits beautifully with AIEMS.

AIEMS isn't trying to minimise cost.

It's trying to maximise engineering return on investment (EROI).

Every recommendation should answer:

What does it cost?

What capability does it unlock?

What engineering effort does it save?

What future options does it create?

Is that value sustainable over the life of the project?

If the answer is compelling, a subscription can be entirely justified.

My Promise for WP2

Going forward, I won't recommend something because it's:

free,

open source,

commercial,

or popular.

I'll recommend it because, based on the evidence, it delivers the best long-term value for Project JARVIS AI at its current stage of evolution.

And don't worry...

I won't come back with a recommendation that starts with:

"For only 2,500 per month..."

I know we're engineering Project JARVIS AI, not the IT budget of a Fortune 500 company.

Instead, every recommendation will include an honest assessment of whether it's appropriate for:

Home / Personal

Family

Professional / Prosumer

Enterprise

That way, the Programme Sponsor can make an informed decision based on value, not just features. I think that will make WP2's recommendations much more practical and much more useful.

"Technology is selected to maximise long-term engineering value, not to minimise immediate cost."

1. Design Intent

The User Experience Platform is not a GUI.

It is the presentation platform for Project JARVIS AI.

It shall:

Present information.

Never own business logic.

Never own system state.

Consume APIs exposed by JARVIS Core.

Provide an intuitive and evolving experience for engineering, family, and future product users.

4. Candidate Platforms

Option 1 - Tauri + React

Strengths

Excellent separation between frontend and backend.

Modern component architecture.

Lightweight runtime.

Strong desktop performance.

Excellent animation capabilities.

Suitable for dashboards and future avatar integration.

Cross-platform without forcing us to use it immediately.

Strong long-term roadmap.

Risks

Requires learning React if not already familiar.

Two technology stacks (Python + React).

Cost

Licence: Free (MIT/Apache)

Runtime: Free

Long-term cost: Low

Engineering productivity: High

Strategic Value

Option 2 - Electron + React

Strengths

Huge ecosystem.

Mature tooling.

Very large community.

Weaknesses

Higher memory usage.

Larger deployment footprint.

Bundled Chromium increases maintenance overhead.

Cost

Free.

Strategic Value

Option 3 - PySide6 (Qt)

Strengths

Single-language workflow (Python).

Mature desktop toolkit.

Native desktop widgets.

Weaknesses

More traditional desktop look and feel.

Rich, modern animations require more effort.

Future web/mobile convergence is weaker.

Cost

Generally free under LGPL for suitable use.

Strategic Value

Option 4 - Flutter Desktop

Strengths

Excellent UI performance.

Beautiful animations.

Good future mobile alignment.

Weaknesses

Separate language (Dart).

Smaller desktop ecosystem for our use case.

Less synergy with our likely backend stack.

Strategic Value

Option 5 - Avalonia

Strengths

Strong .NET desktop framework.

Native feel.

Weaknesses

Introduces another ecosystem (.NET/C#).

Less alignment with our existing Python-first direction.

Strategic Value

Choosing it early reduces uncertainty elsewhere.

2. The AI Landscape Has Changed

Compared to when Project JARVIS AI started, the ecosystem now includes mature local and hybrid AI runtimes. Rather than choosing a single model, I think our evaluation should focus on the AI Runtime Architecture.

This aligns with our existing backlog item:

Provider independence

Local-first operation

Engineering Assessment

Today's decision wasn't simply a name change.

We've actually changed the architectural concept.

GUI meant:

A graphical interface.

UXP means:

The complete User Experience Platform.

That is a broader architectural responsibility.

Therefore, some historical references to "GUI" should remain unchanged because they accurately describe what was meant at the time, while current architecture should adopt the new terminology.

across:

Why This Is Better

Suppose an old ESR says:

"Implement the basic GUI."

That should remain exactly as written because it reflects the historical engineering discussion.

But if MOD-0001 currently says:

GUI

I would recommend changing that to:

User Experience Platform (UXP)

because it reflects our current architectural understanding.

I Also Recommend Updating the OSE

One thing we've learned today is that OSE should reflect the current canonical terminology.

That means the primary node should become:

User Experience Platform (UXP)

with historical aliases such as:

GUI

User Interface

Graphical User Interface

linked as synonyms rather than separate concepts.

This preserves searchability while avoiding fragmentation of the engineering knowledge graph.

Repository Impact

Backlog (EBG-0028 "GUI Evolution Roadmap" may become "UXP Evolution Roadmap")

OSE relationships

Future PVTM relationships

Any engineering specifications that refer to the current architectural component

Importantly, this should be done once, in a controlled way, rather than incrementally introducing mixed terminology.

Recommendation

I recommend we do not interrupt WP2 to perform this alignment.

Instead, we add ESWP-UXP-001 to the ESR-0008 Closure Pack, exactly as we've been doing for other architectural sustainment activities.

That gives us several advantages:

We continue evaluating technologies using the newly approved UXP terminology.

Codex performs the terminology migration in one controlled repository update.

I verify the migration during the next Engineering Synchronisation.

OSE remains consistent with no duplicate concepts.

I think this is another example of the engineering maturity we've developed during ESR-0008. Rather than making ad hoc edits as we go, we recognise the architectural improvement, record it, and execute it as a controlled sustainment activity with full repository and OSE validation.
